## Expected Output Files
- `F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\total_figure_mapping.json`

## Work Instructions
1. Read `fig_table_lnpdb_classified.csv`.
2. Use rows where `manual_select` is `yes` or `maybe` as selected FT items.
3. If `manual_select` is absent, fall back to `need_for_lnpdb` values `yes` or `maybe`.
4. For every selected item, inspect `item_id`, `base_id`, `caption`, and `reason`.
5. Search the paper folder for source image, table, and PDF assets.
6. Treat Marker-extracted `_page_x_Figure_y.jpeg` images as primary candidates only, not ground truth.
7. Map each selected figure/table item to the most likely source image/table path.
8. When possible, record `source_pdf` and 1-based `source_page` for each mapping entry.
9. Infer `source_page` from markdown image/caption page and order when explicit page metadata is unavailable.
10. If the source image is far from the caption, appears to include only part of the figure, or does not match the expected panel count, set `source_quality: "suspect_crop"`.
11. If no plausible image exists, set `source_quality: "missing_image"` and `manual_required: true`.
12. If image and caption appear mismatched, set `source_quality: "caption_image_mismatch"`.
13. If the source image is complete and caption-consistent, set `source_quality: "ok"`.
14. Create `total_figure_mapping.json` in the paper folder root.
15. Follow `agent_workspace/OUTPUT_SCHEMA.md` for the `total_figure_mapping.json` schema.
16. Store paths relative to the paper folder when possible.
17. If uncertain, record `confidence: "low"` or `confidence: "unmatched"` and a short `reason`; do not guess.

## Optional Mapping Fields
- `source_image`
- `source_pdf`
- `source_page`
- `source_quality`
- `fallback_render`
- `selected_source_for_paneling`
- `manual_required`
- `confidence`
- `reason`

python Agent_Task_Runner.py validate --stage 03_figure_mapping --paper-folder "F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3"

## Work Instructions
1. Read `fig_table_lnpdb_classified.csv`.
2. Use rows where `manual_select` is `yes` or `maybe` as selected FT items.
3. If `manual_select` is absent, fall back to `need_for_lnpdb` values `yes` or `maybe`.
4. For every selected item, inspect `item_id`, `base_id`, `caption`, and `reason`.
5. Search the paper folder for source image, table, and PDF assets.
6. Treat Marker-extracted `_page_x_Figure_y.jpeg` images as primary candidates only, not ground truth.
7. Map each selected figure/table item to the most likely source image/table path.
8. When possible, record `source_pdf` and 1-based `source_page` for each mapping entry.
9. Infer `source_page` from markdown image/caption page and order when explicit page metadata is unavailable.
10. If the source image is far from the caption, appears to include only part of the figure, or does not match the expected panel count, set `source_quality: "suspect_crop"`.
11. If no plausible image exists, set `source_quality: "missing_image"` and `manual_required: true`.
12. If image and caption appear mismatched, set `source_quality: "caption_image_mismatch"`.
13. If the source image is complete and caption-consistent, set `source_quality: "ok"`.
14. Create `total_figure_mapping.json` in the paper folder root.
15. Follow `agent_workspace/OUTPUT_SCHEMA.md` for the `total_figure_mapping.json` schema.
16. Store paths relative to the paper folder when possible.
17. If uncertain, record `confidence: "low"` or `confidence: "unmatched"` and a short `reason`; do not guess.

## Optional Mapping Fields
- `source_image`
- `source_pdf`
- `source_page`
- `source_quality`
- `fallback_render`
- `selected_source_for_paneling`
- `manual_required`
- `confidence`
- `reason`

# Output Schema

This file records expected artifact shapes for validation. Existing scripts may include extra columns or keys; agents should tolerate extra fields but require the minimum fields below when the artifact exists.

## `fig_table_inventory.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses with pandas or a standard CSV reader.
- Row count is greater than zero for papers with figures/tables.
- One identifier column exists: `item_id`, `pdf_item_id`, or `item`.
- Useful optional columns: `caption`, `label`, `type`, `page`, `source_file`.

## `fig_table_lnpdb_classified.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- It preserves an FT identifier column.
- It contains some relevance/classification signal.
- After manual review, it should contain `manual_select`.

Useful optional columns:

- `is_lnpdb`
- `lnpdb_class`
- `reason`
- `manual_select`
- `excel_item_id`
- `matched_blocks`
- `matched_block_csv_path`

## `.manual_select_review_done`

Minimum expectations:

- File exists in the paper folder.
- Its content may be empty; existence is the gate for active agent stages.

## `total_figure_mapping.json`

Expected shape:

```json
{
  "source_folder_name": {
    "figure 1": {
      "source_image": "relative/or/absolute/path.png",
      "source_pdf": "relative/or/absolute/source.pdf",
      "source_page": 3,
      "source_quality": "ok",
      "fallback_render": "pdf_page_renders/source_page_003.png",
      "selected_source_for_paneling": "relative/or/absolute/path.png",
      "manual_required": false,
      "confidence": "high",
      "reason": "short rationale",
      "panels": {
        "A": "relative/or/absolute/panel.png"
      }
    }
  }
}
```

Validation rules:

- JSON parses.
- Top-level value is an object.
- At least one nested mapping exists when selected figure/table rows exist.
- Any local path fields found in values should resolve to existing files or be logged as missing.
- `source_image` is a primary candidate, not guaranteed ground truth.
- Optional fallback fields are allowed for Marker crop failures:
  - `source_pdf`
  - `source_page`
  - `source_quality`
  - `fallback_render`
  - `selected_source_for_paneling`
  - `manual_required`
  - `confidence`
  - `reason`
- Allowed `source_quality` values:
  - `ok`
  - `suspect_crop`
  - `missing_image`
  - `caption_image_mismatch`
  - `pdf_page_render_fallback`
  - `manual_required`
- If `fallback_render` or `selected_source_for_paneling` is present and is a local path, it should resolve under the paper folder or as an absolute file path.
- If boundaries or source identity remain uncertain, set `manual_required` to `true` and do not invent panel crops.

## `excel_block_inventory.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- Each row describes one block or table candidate.
- Required useful columns when generated by current code:
  - `excel_file`
  - `excel_sheet`
  - `block_id`
  - `group_id`
  - `element_id`
  - `block_csv_path`
  - `block_meta_path`
  - `block_type`

Validation rules:

- Non-empty `block_csv_path` resolves under the paper folder.
- Block CSV files parse as CSV.
- `Exp_Excel_Blocks/` exists when rows exist.

## `three_core_result_all.json`

Minimum expectations:

- JSON parses.
- Top-level value is a list.
- Rows include `excel_file`, `excel_sheet`, and `result` when produced by current code.

## `separated_panels_gemini/`

Minimum expectations:

- Directory exists when panel separation is performed.
- Contains image files or subfolders with image files.
- Recorded panel paths in mapping JSON exist.

## `excel_mapping.json`

Expected shape:

```json
{
  "figure 1": [
    {
      "pdf_item_id": "figure 1",
      "excel_item_id": "table or block id",
      "excel_file": "source.xlsx",
      "excel_sheet": "Sheet1",
      "block_id": "block id",
      "block_csv_path": "Exp_Excel_Blocks/source/Sheet1/block.csv",
      "reason": "short rationale"
    }
  ]
}
```

Validation rules:

- JSON parses.
- Top-level value is an object.
- Non-empty `block_csv_path` values resolve under the paper folder.

## `excel_mapping_rows.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- Useful columns:
  - `pdf_item_id`
  - `excel_item_id`
  - `excel_file`
  - `excel_sheet`
  - `block_id`
  - `block_csv_path`
  - `reason`

## `compound_inventory_standardized.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- One row per compound/name candidate.
- Useful columns:
  - `compound_id`
  - `Name`
  - `alias`
  - `source_type`
  - `source_path`
  - `Item_ID`
  - `evidence_text`
  - `manual_required`
  - `reason`

## `smiles_resolved.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- Columns include at least one name identifier: `Name` or `compound_id`.
- Columns include at least one SMILES field: `SMILES` or `resolved_smiles`.
- Unresolved compounds should keep a row with blank SMILES and `manual_required=true`.
- Active workflow SMILES may only come from exact LNPDB/reference name or alias matches, curated/local known mappings, text/name/IUPAC deterministic lookup that does not rely on structure images, or explicitly manual-verified curated files.
- Non-empty SMILES must not come from DECIMER, MolScribe, `worker_mol.py`, molecule image crops, image structure recognition, `recognition.py`, or `segmentation.py`.
- Novel pILs should remain blank unless an exact text/reference/manual-curated SMILES entry is present. Use reason `Structure-image-based SMILES extraction is disabled; no exact text/reference SMILES was available.` when appropriate.
- Current unified extraction outputs do not project SMILES from this file. Stage 06/07 force all formulation/component SMILES output columns blank.

## `unified_extraction.csv`

Expected shape:

- Long-format CSV with one row per figure/table item, formulation/condition context, and experimental metric/value context when reliable mapped Excel/source-data values exist.
- `metric_type`, `original_values`, `aggregated_value`, `unit`, and `replicate_type` may be populated only from mapped Excel/source-data blocks with Excel provenance.
- Graph image digitization, pixel/axis extraction, bar-height estimation, heatmap color estimation, caption-only numeric inference, and hallucinated values are disabled.
- `IL_SMILES`, `HL_SMILES`, `CHL_SMILES`, `PEG_SMILES`, and `Fifth_component_SMILES` are compatibility columns and must remain blank in current unified outputs. Preserve component name and molar-ratio columns.
- It replaces separate experimental condition, formulation, and Excel-backed value extraction outputs for the active workflow.

Required columns:

- `Paper_ID`
- `Item_ID`
- `visual_type`
- `source_type`
- `source_image`
- `source_pdf`
- `source_page`
- `selected_source_for_paneling`
- `excel_file`
- `excel_sheet`
- `block_id`
- `block_csv_path`
- `Aqueous_buffer`
- `Dialysis_buffer`
- `Mixing_method`
- `Model`
- `Model_type`
- `Model_target`
- `Route_of_administration`
- `Cargo`
- `Cargo_type`
- `Dose_ug_nucleicacid`
- `Experiment_method`
- `Experiment_batching`
- `formulation_id`
- `Formulation_Name`
- `IL_name`
- `IL_SMILES`
- `IL_molarratio`
- `HL_name`
- `HL_SMILES`
- `HL_molarratio`
- `CHL_name`
- `CHL_SMILES`
- `CHL_molarratio`
- `PEG_name`
- `PEG_SMILES`
- `PEG_molarratio`
- `Fifth_component_name`
- `Fifth_component_SMILES`
- `Fifth_component_molarratio`
- `IL_to_nucleicacid_massratio`
- `condition_1_name`
- `condition_1_value`
- `condition_2_name`
- `condition_2_value`
- `condition_3_name`
- `condition_3_value`
- `condition_4_name`
- `condition_4_value`
- `metric_type`
- `original_values`
- `aggregated_value`
- `unit`
- `replicate_type`
- `evidence_text`
- `evidence_image`
- `evidence_excel`
- `confidence`
- `manual_required`
- `reason`

Validation rules:

- CSV parses.
- Required columns exist.
- `Item_ID` is non-empty for every row.
- `metric_type`, `original_values`, `aggregated_value`, `unit`, and `replicate_type` may be populated only when the value comes from a reliable mapped Excel/source-data block.
- Populated value rows must include Excel/source-data provenance in `evidence_excel`, `excel_file`, `excel_sheet`, or `block_csv_path`.
- `original_values` and `aggregated_value` should be numeric-like or pipe-separated numeric-like values unless a future categorical metric is explicitly enabled.
- Do not populate value columns from graph images, pixel/axis extraction, visual estimation, heatmap colors, captions alone, or hallucinated values.
- `IL_SMILES`, `HL_SMILES`, `CHL_SMILES`, `PEG_SMILES`, and `Fifth_component_SMILES` must be blank. Blank SMILES are not a manual-review issue in the current workflow.
- Condition fields must be concise scalar LNPDB-style values. Prose belongs only in `evidence_text`.
- Multiple panel/model/route/dose/method contexts must not be merged into one scalar field; split rows by panel/item/block context or leave uncertain fields blank with `manual_required=true`.
- Missing condition/formulation fields should remain blank with `manual_required=true` where review is needed.
- Optional LNPDB reference examples and human-curated guide definitions may guide normalization, but output fields should still contain concise scalar values supported by paper evidence.
- Prefer column-specific existing LNPDB examples over generic examples. `Experiment_method` may include assay+readout specificity, e.g. `flow_cytometry_CD8_T_cells`, when needed to preserve panel identity.
- `confidence` and `reason` should explain nontrivial extraction decisions.

## `unified_extraction_review_flags.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- Useful columns:
  - `Paper_ID`
  - `Item_ID`
  - `block_id`
  - `field`
  - `issue`
  - `severity`
  - `reason`
- Rows identify manual review needs such as missing metadata, formulation/value mismatch, ambiguous Excel value mapping, or missing evidence. Blank output SMILES are expected and are not review flags by themselves.

## `unified_extraction_final.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- Preserves `unified_extraction.csv` rows after final normalization/review.
- Keeps provenance columns needed to audit source evidence.

## `unified_extraction_lnpdb_like.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- Contains LNPDB-facing value rows derived from `unified_extraction_final.csv`.
- Contains deterministic stable `row_id` values such as `<Paper_ID>_R000001`.
- Does not repeat large source evidence text fields; evidence is normalized into the source evidence and figure evidence map tables.
- Does not invent missing scientific values during projection.

## `unified_extraction_source_evidence.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- One row per unique evidence phrase/source span/source object.
- Contains deterministic `evidence_id` values such as `<Paper_ID>_E000001`.
- Contains compact `evidence_summary`, pipe-separated `evidence_sentence_ids`, and optional `evidence_sentence_texts`.
- `evidence_sentence_ids` references `markdown_sentence_index/markdown_sentence_index_all.csv` using global IDs such as `QS_2026:S000145`.
- `evidence_text_exact` may be blank or compact when sentence IDs are available; it is retained for backward compatibility and fallback review only.
- Includes source provenance fields such as `source_pdf`, `source_page`, `source_image`, `evidence_excel`, `excel_file`, `excel_sheet`, `block_id`, and `block_csv_path`.
- Includes placeholder PDF/image bbox and character offset columns for later UI highlighting; these may be blank.

## `markdown_sentence_index/`

Minimum expectations:

- Contains numbered sentence indexes generated from source markdown files, excluding markdown table regions and generated/output folders.
- Per-source outputs: `<source_md_id>.sentences.md`, `<source_md_id>.sentences.csv`, and `<source_md_id>.sentences.json`.
- Combined outputs: `markdown_sentence_index_all.csv` and `markdown_sentence_index_manifest.json`.
- Sentence IDs are deterministic per source markdown file: `[S000001]`, `[S000002]`, etc.
- `global_sentence_id` values use `<source_md_id>:<sentence_id>`, for example `QS_2026:S000145`.
- Table rows such as pipe tables and table separator rows are excluded by design.

## `paper_source_context.json`

Minimum expectations:

- Describes the selected `paper_folder` as one paper-level document package with one `Paper_ID`.
- Lists source documents discovered under the folder with `source_doc_id`, `source_document_type`, `source_md_path`, optional `source_pdf_path`, `source_md_id`, `title`, `doi`, and `priority`.
- `source_document_type` values include `main_article`, `supplementary_information`, `source_data`, `reporting_summary`, or `unknown`.
- Global methods/protocol evidence may cross source documents within this same paper package, but evidence must not be merged across different paper folders.

## `unified_extraction_figure_evidence_map.csv`

Minimum expectations:

- CSV parses.
- Figure/item-level bridge table connecting each `evidence_id` to the LNPDB scientific condition, formulation, and Excel-backed metric/value columns it supports.
- Required fields include `Paper_ID`, `Item_ID`, `evidence_id`, `supported_columns`, `supported_column_count`, `supported_row_ids`, `supported_formulation_ids`, `support_scope`, optional `evidence_sentence_ids`, optional `evidence_summary`, `confidence`, `manual_required`, and `reason`.
- `supported_columns` contains pipe-separated LNPDB scientific condition/formulation columns and Excel-backed value columns only. Semicolon-separated legacy files remain readable. It must not contain administrative, provenance, or reason columns.
- Excel value evidence may support `metric_type`, `original_values`, `aggregated_value`, `unit`, and `replicate_type` when `evidence_source_type` is `excel_block_value` or `source_data_excel`.
- At least one evidence map row should exist for each `Item_ID` with non-empty scientific condition/formulation/value columns, unless the item is a manual-review placeholder.
- This is intentionally not a per-cell evidence table. UI lookup can use: `row_id + Item_ID + column_name -> figure_evidence_map rows whose supported_columns include column_name -> evidence_id -> source_evidence evidence_sentence_ids -> markdown_sentence_index`.
- `methods_global` evidence may support rows whose figures came from a different source document in the same paper package. For example, main-article LNP preparation sentences can support supplementary LNP rows with populated `Aqueous_buffer`, `Dialysis_buffer`, and `Mixing_method`.
- Global LNP preparation evidence should not be attached to PBS-only, untreated, free drug, or free mRNA rows unless the row is explicitly an LNP/pLNP formulation row.

F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:49:synthesize an indoximod pIL, we first generated an intermediate prodrug structure, Nitro-SS-AA (Supplementary Fig. 1). As shown in Supplementary Fig. 1, the hydroxyls of 2-hydroxyethyl disulfide were first protected by 4-nitrophenyl carbonochloridate to generate reactive carbonates (Supplementary Figs. 1 and 4) for a subsequent reaction
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:51:with indoximod (1-methyl-D-tryptophan) to obtain 4-Nitro-SS-AA (Supplementary Fig. 5). The presence of a disulfide linkage in this structure enables intracellular release of indoximod via GSH cleavage. Next, Nitro-SS-AA was reacted with seven amine heads to generate seven prodrug polyamine heads, and all structures were confirmed by
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:53:NMR and electrospray ionization mass spectrometry (ESI-MS) spectra (Fig. [1a](#page-1-0) and Supplementary Figs. 2 and 6–19). These structures were then reacted with 1 of the 3 epoxide-terminated alkyl tails for 12 h at 70 °C to yield a library of 21 pILs (Supplementary Fig. 3), and all structures of pILs were confirmed by NMR and high resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) spectra (Supplementary Figs. 20–39). In this study, pILs are denoted by a specific nomenclature: P2A to DAB refers to the seven amine head groups, SS refers to the presence of a disulfide linkage, AA refers to the presence of 1-methyl-D-tryptophan, and C10/ C12/C14 refers to the length of the alkyl tails in the ionizable lipid. For example, 'G0-SS-AA-C12' has a G0 amine head, a disulfide linkage, 1-methyl-D-tryptophan and C12 alkyl tails.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:55:Next, the pILs were combined with 1,2-dioleoyl-sn-glycero-3-p hosphoethanolamine (DOPE), 1,2-dimyristoyl-rac-glycero-3-m ethoxypolyethylene glycol-2000 (DMG-PEG) and cholesterol in ethanol and pipette-mixed with 1-methylpseudouridine (m1ψ)-modified firefly luciferase (FLuc) mRNA to formulate 21 pLNPs. The molar ratio of pIL/cholesterol/DOPE/DMG-PEG was kept constant at 50:38.5:10:1.5 (Fig. [2a](#page-2-0)). The pLNP library was screened in HepG2 cells, an immortalized human hepatocellular carcinoma cell line. Among the 21 pILs, G0-based pILs had the highest transfection efficiency in vitro among the 7 amine head groups (Supplementary Fig. 47). The top eight pILs were advanced for study of in vivo efficiency following intravenous administration of pLNPs encapsulating FLuc mRNA at a dose of 2.5 μg per mouse. All pLNPs primarily transfected the liver. G0-based pLNPs (G0-SS-AA-C10/ C12/C14) exhibited higher expression of reporter protein compared with other LNPs, including LNPs containing the ionizable lipid MC3, which is used in US Food and Drug Administration (FDA)-approved formulations (Supplementary Fig. 48).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:57:Then, we verified that pILs could be used to transfect an additional cancer cell line. We repeated a similar workflow, this time using MC38 cells, a mouse colon adenocarcinoma cell line (Fig. [2a\)](#page-2-0). Treatment of MC38 cells in vitro with our library of pLNPs encapsulating FLuc mRNA showed that G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP had the highest transfection efficiency (Fig. [2b\)](#page-2-0). All pLNPs exhibited low cytotoxicity in vitro (Supplementary Fig. 49). To emphasize the structural advantage of the pIL, we also designed a control lipid, termed G0-6C-AA-C12, that could not release drug by substituting the disulfide linkage with carbon–carbon linkage (Supplementary Figs. 40–46). Interestingly, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP facilitated 1.5-fold greater transfection of MC38 cells than LNPs formulated with a structural analogue that lacked the disulfide linkage (G0-6C-AA-C12) (Supplementary Fig. 50). Since G0-6C-AA-C12 has a carbon–carbon bond instead of a disulfide linkage, it is unable to release indoximod in the cytoplasm, implying that the prodrug itself within the pIL potentially aids in pLNP-mediated mRNA transfection.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:59:The top three pILs (G0-SS-AA-C10, G0-SS-AA-C12 and G0-SS-AA-C14) from our initial screens were advanced for further study. Given our interest in applying pLNPs for solid tumour therapy, we next assessed pIL efficiency relative to two control lipids (G0-6C-AA-C12 and MC3) after intratumoural injection into mouse MC38 tumours. In brief, LNPs encapsulating FLuc mRNA at a dose of 2.5 μg per mouse were administered to MC38 tumour-bearing mice, and luciferase expression was quantified 6 h post-injection using bioluminescence imaging (Fig. [2c](#page-2-0) and Supplementary Fig. 51). G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP facilitated greater whole-body mRNA transfection compared with MC3 LNP (Supplementary Fig. 51). Ex vivo quantification of luminescence intensity specifically within MC38 tumours demonstrated that G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP outperformed other pLNPs and MC3 LNP (Fig. [2c\)](#page-2-0). Moreover, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP induced low off-target transfection of the liver, while MC3 LNP induced substantially higher liver transfection (Supplementary Fig. 51). On the basis of these data, G0-SS-AA-C12 was selected as the lead pIL for further study.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:61:Purified G0-SS-AA-C12 or G0-6C-AA-C12 were combined with standard excipients and mixed with FLuc mRNA using a microfluidic device to generate pLNPs for physiochemical characterization. Transmission electron microscopy images showed uniform, multilamellar, complex spherical morphologies of both G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP with hydrodynamic diameters of 43 nm and 78 nm, polydispersity index values of 0.12 and 0.26, and slightly positive surface charges (Supplementary Figs. 52 and 53). FLuc mRNA encapsulation efficiency (EE) of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP was determined to be 91.2% and 89.6%, respectively, and their pKa values were approximately 6.67 and 6.50, respectively (Supplementary Figs. 52–54). Next, we compared the cellular uptake of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP and MC3 LNP via 3,3′-dioctadecyloxacarbocyanine perchlorate (DiO) labelling. Confocal imaging showed greater intracellular uptake of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP relative to MC3 and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNPs in MC38 cells (Supplementary Fig. 55). Moreover, we assessed the endosomal escape of the lead pLNP. A higher percentage of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP achieved endosomal escape compared with MC3 LNP in MC38 cells (Fig. [2d](#page-2-0)), demonstrating a potential mechanism for enhanced efficiency for the lead delivery platform.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:65:Encouraged by the strong in vitro and in vivo efficiency of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP, we next assessed its potential to deliver IL-12 mRNA. We formulated G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP and MC3 LNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA and treated MC38 cells at a dose of 0.5 μg ml−1 of mRNA. After 16 h, both the supernatant and cells were collected for enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA)-based analysis of extracellular and intracellular IL-12 protein expression (Fig. [3a\)](#page-3-0). Although both extracellular and intracellular IL-12 protein was detected in all three treatment groups, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP facilitated 44-fold greater extracellular IL-12 protein and 8.6-fold greater intracellular IL-12 protein than MC3 LNP (Fig. [3b,c](#page-3-0)). Moreover, treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP resulted in 2.8-fold greater extracellular IL-12 protein and 4.4-fold greater intracellular IL-12 protein than G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP (Fig. [3b,c\)](#page-3-0). Flow cytometry (Supplementary Fig. 56) and confocal imaging confirmed strong intracellular IL-12 expression in MC38 cells after G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP treatment (Fig. [3d,e\)](#page-3-0). We then investigated the drug release profile of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP. Specifically, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNPs

F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:83:We subsequently explored the potential of GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP to promote T cell activation and limit T cell exhaustion via an in vitro serial culture assay (Supplementary Figs. 60-63). In brief, MC38 cells were treated with PBS (negative control) or GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating either IL-12 mRNA or FLuc mRNA. After 24 h, the supernatant of each group was collected and used to culture primary mouse CD3<sup>+</sup>T cells. Following 48 h of culture, T cells were collected and analysed for production of key effector cytokines, including tumour necrosis factor-α  $(TNF-\alpha)$ , interferon-y (IFNy) and granzyme B (Supplementary Fig. 60a). Cytokine levels were measured via intracellular cytokine staining and flow cytometry. CD3<sup>+</sup> T cells cultured in supernatant from MC38 cells treated with GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP exhibited notably higher levels of TNF- $\alpha$ , IFNy and granzyme B compared with those cultured in control supernatants (Supplementary Fig. 60). Moreover, treatment with GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP enhanced cytokine production in CD4<sup>+</sup> and CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells (Supplementary Figs. 61 and 62) and enhanced T cell proliferation (Supplementary Fig. 67).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:85:The capacity of GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP to mitigate T cell exhaustion was evaluated by co-culturing primary OT-I CD3<sup>+</sup> T cells with ovalbumin-modified MC38 (MC38-OVA) cancer cells at a 1:1 effector-to-target (E:T) ratio. GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP and GO-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP were added at 0.5 µg per well for 3 days. Cells treated with PBS served as a negative control. Every 3 days, equivalent numbers of MC38-OVA cells and corresponding amounts of mRNApLNP were added, for a total of 5 cycles, to mimic chronic T cell stimulation within the TME. After the final stimulation, cells were collected and analysed via flow cytometry for CD69 and CD25 expression, markers of T cell activation, and for PD-1 expression, a marker of T cell exhaustion<sup>5,46</sup> (Fig. 3h). Treatment with GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP upregulated CD69 and CD25 on OT-I CD3+T cells (Fig. 3i,j and Supplementary Figs. 64a,b and 65) and downregulated PD-1 (Fig. 3k and Supplementary Figs. 64c and 66) relative to control groups. Interestingly, GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA mediated greater suppression of PD-1 expression than pLNP encapsulating FLuc mRNA (Fig. 3k and Supplementary Fig. 64c). Together, these results imply a synergistic mechanism between the released indoximod and translated IL-12 in resisting T cell exhaustion.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:89:We next evaluated the therapeutic efficacy of GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP in an MC38 tumour mouse model. To highlight the synergistic effect of this platform, composed of both a small-molecule IDO inhibitor and therapeutic IL-12 mRNA, we prepared two control LNPs. GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating FLuc mRNA was used to assess the effect of IDO inhibitor only, while GO-6C-AA-C12 LNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA was used to evaluate the anti-tumour efficacy of IL-12 mRNA only (Supplementary Fig. 68). After subcutaneous injection of MC38 cells into the right flank of mice, GO-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, GO-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP or GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP were administered intratumourally at an mRNA dose of 2.5 µg per mouse on days 6, 8 and 10 (Fig. 4a). PBS was administered via the same procedure as a negative control. GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA suppressed tumour

F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:93:growth (Fig. 4b), inducing compelete tumour regression in all treated mice within 31 days of model initiation (Fig. 4b,c). In comparison, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating FLuc mRNA and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA only partially inhibited tumour growth (Fig. 4b,c). All LNPs were well tolerated with no effect on body weight (Fig. 4d) or major organ histology (Supplementary Fig. 69) relative to control mice. Finally, PBS-treated mice succumbed to tumour burden before day 45, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP treatment moderately increased survival, and all G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP-treated mice survived to our study endpoint (Fig. 4e).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:95:To further characterize the importance of co-formulation of the IDO inhibitor with IL-12 mRNA in a single LNP, we evaluated three additional control groups: free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP with free IDO inhibitor (Supplementary Fig. 70a). GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP demonstrated superior anti-tumour efficacy compared to these control groups. Neither free IL-12 mRNA nor free IDO inhibitor alone notably suppressed tumour growth (Supplementary Fig. 70b,e). G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP plus free IDO inhibitor only partially inhibited tumour progression with mean tumour volumes rising rapidly after day 22 (Supplementary Fig. 70b,c). By contrast, GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP completely suppressed tumour growth, and achieved complete regression of all tumours within 32 days in this study (Supplementary Fig. 70c,e). All mice in the GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP-treated group survived to study endpoint (Supplementary Fig. 70d). Splenic CD4+, CD8+ and regulatory T cell response was also assessed for each of these groups on day 12 (Supplementary Fig. 71a). Notably, GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP elicited a robust CD8<sup>+</sup>T cell response and diminished the number of CD4<sup>+</sup> and regulatory T cells compared with control groups (Supplementary Figs. 71-73). Overall, these data confirmed that co-formulation of both an IDO inhibitor and IL-12 mRNA in a single LNP is integral to induction of a potent immune response and anti-tumour efficacy.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:97:We next investigated the anti-tumour mechanism of G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP. Given that IL-12 is known to activate cells in both the innate and adaptive immune compartment and the spleen plays a major role in immunoregulation  $^{47,48}$ , we more specifically explored the effect of pLNPs specifically on splenic T cell populations in the MC38 tumour mouse model. As observed previously, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP biased splenic T cells towards CD8 $^{+}$  populations and away from CD4 $^{+}$  populations relative to control groups (Fig. 4f–h). More specifically, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP enhanced effector memory (CD44 $^{+}$ CD62L $^{-}$ ) and central memory (CD44 $^{+}$ CD62L $^{+}$ ) CD4 $^{+}$  and CD8 $^{+}$  splenic T cell populations (Fig. 4i–l and Supplementary Figs. 74 and 75), and reduced the presence of splenic regulatory T cells (CD4 $^{+}$ CD25 $^{+}$ FOXP3 $^{+}$ ) (Fig. 4m and Supplementary Figs. 76 and 77). Together, these results demonstrate that G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP achieves anti-tumour efficacy via immune priming towards an effector T cell phenotype while also generating long-term immune memory.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:105:was prepared and treated with either PBS, GO-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, GO-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP or GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP as described previously. On day 12, tumours were collected and digested into single-cell suspensions (Fig. 5a). GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP increased CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:107:within the TME (Fig. 5b) relative to control groups, likely owing to IL-12-mediated recruitment and stimulation of T cell effector functions. There was no significant difference between the number of CD4 $^+$  cells between treatment groups (Fig. 5c). In line with the observed increase
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:111:in T cell infiltration, intracellular levels of TNF-α, IFNγ and granzyme B in CD3<sup>+</sup> T cells were elevated after treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP (Supplementary Fig. 78a,b,d–i). Relative to PBS-treated tumours, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP downregulated PD-1 expression on CD4+ and CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells, demonstrating the influence of the released IDO inhibitor (Fig. [5d,e\)](#page-6-0). G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP further decreased CD4<sup>+</sup> and CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell exhaustion in the TME, demonstrating further synergy in therapeutic function between IL-12 and IDO inhibitor (Fig. [5d,e](#page-6-0) and Supplementary Figs. 81 and 82). G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP also significantly elevated pro-inflammatory cytokines IL-6, TNF-α and IFNγ levels in tumour tissues (Fig. [5f,g](#page-6-0) and Supplementary Fig. 78a–c). Finally, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP maintained performance relative to tumours treated with free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP with free IDO inhibitor (Supplementary Figs. 79 and 80), highlighting the importance of IDO inhibitor and IL-12 mRNA co-formulation. Thus, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP enhances effector T cell infiltration and reduces T cell exhaustion in the TME via synergistic mechanisms, leading to the anti-tumour efficacy observed in the MC38 tumour mouse model.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:113:To understand the impact of IDO inhibitor and IL-12 release from pLNPs on the gene expression profile of cells within the TME, we performed bulk RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) on collected MC38 tumours (Fig. [5a,h–o](#page-6-0)). IL-12 exerts many of its downstream effects via IFN[γ49.](#page-11-27) Thus, it was unsurprising that both G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP- and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP-treated tumours had an upregulation of IFNγ transcripts by 6.3-fold and 2.1-fold, respectively, relative to PBS-treated tumours (Fig. [5i](#page-6-0)), which was consistent with high IFNγ levels in tumour tissue (Fig. [5g\)](#page-6-0). Twenty-eight pro-inflammatory cytokine genes were significantly enriched following treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP (Fig. [5j](#page-6-0) and Supplementary Fig. 83a), while G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP induced expression of 11 and 4 genes, respectively (Supplementary Figs. 84 and 85). At a macro level, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP shifted the gene expression profile of the TME from 'cold' to 'hot', evidenced by upregulation of leukocyte chemokines and pathways responsible for T cell priming, dendritic cell (DC) activation, nucleotide-binding oligomerization domain (NOD)-like receptor signalling, cancer cell apoptosis, and T cell and natural killer (NK) cell activation (Fig. [5j–o](#page-6-0)). By contrast, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP induced only modest changes in immune-response genes (Supplementary Figs. 83b,c, 84 and 85). These data imply that G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNPs modulate the TME by exerting immunostimulatory effects that lead to the anti-tumour efficacy of this platform.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:119:<span id="page-8-0"></span>**Fig. 6 | Intratumoural injection of G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP elicits effective long-term anti-tumour response and drives regression of distal tumours. a**, Schematic depicting in vivo MC38 tumour rechallenge. Mice bearing ~100 mm<sup>3</sup> MC38 tumours were injected with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP on days 6, 8 and 10, resulting in tumour elimination by day 30. Then, MC38 cells were subcutaneously reinjected into the same position to monitor tumour regrowth and mouse survival. Mice naive to pLNP treatment served as a control group. **b**, Mean tumour volume from two independent experiments. **c**, Spider plots of individual tumour growth curves of each mouse in the treatment group and control group. **d**, Survival curve of mice for the treatment group compared with the control group. **e**, Body weight changes of mice in the treatment group and control group. The biologically independent samples in **b**–**d** were *n* = 5 for the control group and *n* = 8 for the treated group. **f**, Scheme depicting the bilateral tumour model. Mice were treated with PBS, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:121:LNP plus free IDO inhibitor, or G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP at an mRNA dose of 2.5 μg per mouse on days 6, 8 and 10 (Supplementary Fig. 86a). Treatment with PBS served as a negative control. Treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP or G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP with free IDO inhibitor inhibited tumour growth (Supplementary Fig. 86b). In comparison, free IL-12 mRNA and free IDO inhibitor failed to exhibit anti-tumour function relative to the PBS-treated group (Supplementary Fig. 86c). Long-term monitoring of tumour progression and mouse survival showed that while LNPs encapsulating IL-12 mRNA partially suppressed tumour burden, they were unable to eradicate tumours (Supplementary Fig. 86d).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:123:We next assessed systemic toxicity of the seven distinct treatment groups. Serum was collected on day 12 for assessment of cytokine levels, liver function markers and pro-inflammatory cytokines (Supplementary Fig. 87a). All LNPs encapsulating IL-12 mRNA elevated serum levels of IL-6, TNF-α and IFNγ after intravenous injection (Supplementary Fig. 87b–d), while treatment with free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor and G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc LNP resulted in serum cytokine levels comparable to the negative control (Supplementary Fig. 87b–d). Similarly, all LNPs encapsulating IL-12 mRNA increased levels of the liver enzymes aspartate aminotransferase (AST) and alanine aminotransferase (ALT) compared with the PBS group (Supplementary Fig. 88). By contrast, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP administered via intratumoural injection did not increase serum IL-6 and TNF-α relative to PBS treatment (Supplementary Fig. 87e–g), and these cytokine levels were 3.4-fold and 2.2-fold lower, respectively, than those after intravenous administration of pLNP (Supplementary Fig. 87h,i). No significant hepatotoxicity was observed after intratumoural injection of any treatment (Supplementary Fig. 89). Together, although pLNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA has moderate anti-tumour efficacy after intravenous injection, systemic toxicity limits the potential of the platform via systemic administration. By contrast, intratumoural injection of pLNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA boasts significant anti-cancer efficacy with minimal systemic toxicity.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:135:We next probed whether the global immunomodulatory effect of G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP could drive the regression of tumours distal to the primary tumour injection site. To test this, mice were inoculated with two subcutaneous MC38 tumours, one on each flank. Then, only the right tumour was injected with either PBS (negative control), G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP or G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP at an mRNA dose of 2.5 μg per mouse on days 6, 8 and 10 (Fig. [6f\)](#page-8-0). G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP induced notable regression of not only the treated tumour but also the contralateral tumour (Fig. [6g,h\)](#page-8-0). All mice treated with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP survived to the terminal endpoint, nearly 3 months after tumour inoculation (Fig. [6i\)](#page-8-0). By contrast, both flank tumours of G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP-treated mice showed rapid growth and resulted in minimal survival benefit relative to PBS-treated mice, demonstrating that IDO inhibitor alone is not sufficient for therapeutic efficacy (Fig. [6h,i](#page-8-0)). For the G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP-treated mice, untreated distal tumour growth was not inhibited (Fig. [6h](#page-8-0)), limiting long-term survival without a cleavable IDO inhibitor (Fig. [6i\)](#page-8-0). No body weight changes were observed in all treatment groups, demonstrating the biocompatibility of pLNP platforms (Fig. [6j\)](#page-8-0). Finally, we explored changes in T cell populations within the contralateral TME via flow cytometry. Akin to the perturbation of T cell populations observed within the primary tumour (Fig. [5b](#page-6-0)), treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP resulted in greater CD8+ T cells in the contralateral tumour relative to control groups (Fig. [6k,l](#page-8-0) and Supplementary Fig. 90). Of note, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP resulted in lower numbers of CD4+ T cells in the contralateral tumour relative to control groups (Fig. [6k,l](#page-8-0) and Supplementary Fig. 90), likely resulting in effective tumour regression owing to reduction of tumour-protective regulatory T cells. Together, these results imply that both IDO inhibitor release and IL-12 expression are necessary within pLNP for elimination of distal tumours and maximal therapeutic efficacy of this platform.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:141:Intravenous administration of G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNPs demonstrated significant anti-tumour efficacy (Supplementary Fig. 86) but was accompanied by systemic toxicity, including cytokine release syndrome and hepatotoxicity in mice (Supplementary Figs. 87 and 88). To mitigate these adverse effects, several strategies could be implemented. For example, incorporating microRNA-122 target sequences (miR-122T) into the 3′-untranslated region of IL-12 mRNA may substantially reduce hepatic IL-12 expressio[n52](#page-11-30). Alternatively, the use of pro-IL-12 mRNA could generate an inactive precursor that is specifically activated within the TME. Conjugating a tumour-targeting antibody to pLNPs may further enhance tumour localization while minimizing hepatic transfection. Finally, co-administration of inhibitors targeting IL-6, TNF-α or IFNγ could reduce IL-12-induced toxicity without compromising anti-tumour efficacy.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\QS_2026\QS_2026.md:143:Although systemically administered immunotherapies (that is, checkpoint blockade, bispecific antibodies and adoptive T cell transfer) have had clinical success[53](#page-11-31), especially in haematologic cancer[s54,](#page-12-0) the majority of solid tumours have remained refractory to these therapies. For these difficult-to-treat cancers, intratumoural immunotherapies, a strategy that involves directly injecting immunostimulatory agents into tumour sites, are currently being tested in clinical trial[s55–](#page-12-1)[57](#page-12-2). Advantages of this approach include overcoming barriers of drug biodistribution to tumours and the ability to achieve high local concentrations of drug with lower systemic exposure. Indeed, while we observed systemic toxicity after intravenous administration of pLNPs encapsulating IL-12 mRNA (Supplementary Figs. 86 and 87), intratumoural administration of this platform elicited potent anti-cancer activity with a strong safety profile (Supplementary Fig. 87).

F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:124:*Synthesis of Nitro-SS-Nitro*. 4-Nitrophenyl carbonochloridate (2.4 equiv.), triethylamine (3 equiv.) and 50 mL of THF were added into a reaction flask. Then 2-hydroxyethyl disulfide (1 equiv.) was dissolved in 50 mL THF and slowly added into the flask. The mixture was stirred at room temperature overnight. Upon completion, the reaction mixture was filtered to remove undissolved substance and collect filtrates. After removing all the solvents on a rotary evaporator, the residues were further purified using a CombiFlash Rf-200i on silica gel using hexane:ethyl acetate (v:v = 6:1) as eluent to afford Nitro-SS-Nitro (yield: 80%). 1H NMR (400 MHz, CDCl3, Supplementary Fig. 4): δ 8.30 (d, *J* = 9.2 Hz, 4H), 7.41 (d, *J* = 9.2 Hz, 4H), 4.59 (t, *J* = 6.5 Hz, 4H), 3.11 (t, *J* = 6.5 Hz, 4H).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:126:*Synthesis of Nitro-SS-AA*. Nitro-SS-Nitro (1.2 equiv.) and triethylamine (1.5 equiv.) were dissolved in 20 mL THF in a reaction flask and stirred at 0 oC. Then 1-methyl-*D*-tryptophan (1 equiv.) was dissolved in a mixture of THF and NaHCO3 aqueous (v:v = 1:3) and slowly added into the reaction flask. The reaction mixture was allowed to warm to room temperature under stirring for 3 h. Upon completion, THF was removed under reduced pressure. The residues were poured into 100 mL ethyl acetate and washed with 50 mL saturated NaCl solution three times. The organic layer was collected and dried over anhydrous Na2SO4, filtered and evaporated to dryness under reduced pressure. The residues were further purified by silica gel column chromatography using hexane:ethyl acetate = 1:1 as eluent to afford Nitro-SS-AA (yield: 52%). 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-*d*6, Supplementary Fig. 5A): δ 8.32 (d, *J* = 9.2 Hz, 2H), 7.59 – 7.47 (m, 4H), 7.36 (s, 1H), 7.17 – 7.10 (m, 2H), 7.07 – 6.97 (m, 1H), 4.47 (t, *J* = 6.1 Hz, 1H), 4.27 – 4.08 (m, 3H), 3.72 (s, 3H), 3.20 – 2.92 (m, 6H). ESI-MS (m/z, Supplementary Fig. 5B): Calcd for [M+Na]+: 586.1, found: 585.8; Calcd for [M+H]+: 564.1, found: 563.8.

F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:188:*Synthesis of Nitro-6C-Nitro*. 4-Nitrophenyl carbonochloridate (2.4 equiv.), triethylamine (3 equiv.) and 50 mL THF were added into a reaction flask. Then 1,6-hexanediol (1 equiv.) was dissolved in 50 mL THF and slowly added into the flask. The mixture was stirred at room temperature overnight. Upon completion, the reaction mixture was filtered to remove undissolved substance and collect filtrates. After removing all the solvents on a rotary evaporator, the residues were further purified by combiflash on silica gel using hexane:ethyl acetate (v:v = 6:1) as eluent to afford Nitro-6C-Nitro (yield: 72%). 1H NMR (400 MHz, CDCl3, Supplementary Fig. 41): δ 8.34 – 8.26 (m, 4H), 7.44 – 7.37 (m, 4H), 4.33 (d, *J* = 6.5 Hz, 4H), 1.83 (p, *J* = 6.7 Hz, 4H), 1.61 – 1.44 (m, 4H).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:190:*Synthesis of Nitro-6C-AA*. Nitro-6C-Nitro (1.2 equiv.) and triethylamine (1.5 equiv.) were dissolved in 20 mL THF in a reaction flask and stirred at 0 oC. Then 1-methyl-*D*-tryptophan (1 equiv.) was dissolved in a mixture of THF and NaHCO3 aqueous (v:v = 1:3) and slowly added into the reaction flask. The reaction mixture was allowed to warm to room temperature under stirring for 3 h. Upon completion, THF was removed under reduced pressure. The residues were poured into 100 mL ethyl acetate and washed with 50 mL saturated NaCl solution three times. The organic layer was collected and dried over anhydrous Na2SO4, filtered and evaporated to dryness under reduced pressure. The residues were further purified by silica gel column chromatography using hexane:ethyl acetate = 1:1 as eluent to afford Nitro-6C-AA (yield: 48%). 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-*d*6, Supplementary Fig. 42A) δ 8.31 (d, *J* = 9.1 Hz, 2H), 7.58 – 7.51 (m, 3H), 7.35 (dd, *J* = 14.5, 8.1 Hz, 2H), 7.16 – 7.08 (m, 2H), 7.01 (t, *J* = 7.4 Hz, 1H), 4.26 – 4.15 (m, 3H), 3.89 (q, *J* = 6.7 Hz, 2H), 3.72 (s, 3H), 3.13 (d, *J* = 4.7 Hz, 1H), 3.02 – 2.96 (m, 1H), 1.65 (d, *J* = 7.2 Hz, 2H), 1.52 (t, *J* = 6.8 Hz, 2H), 0.96 (q, *J* = 3.6 Hz, 4H). 13C NMR (101 MHz, DMSO-*d*6, Supplementary Fig. 42B) δ 175.55, 157.76, 155.79, 152.92, 144.91, 135.93, 129.14, 125.42, 123.51, 121.06, 118.85, 110.06, 67.70, 62.33, 56.86, 32.74, 28.28, 25.36. ESI-MS (m/z, Supplementary Fig. 43): Calcd for [M+Na]+: 550.2, found: 549.9; Calcd for [M+H]+: 528.2, found: 528.0.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:192:*Synthesis of G0-6C-AA*. G0 (3 equiv.), triethylamine (1.5 equiv.) and 10 mL THF were added into a reaction flask and stirred at 0 oC. Then 4-Nitro-6C-AA (1 equiv.) dissolved in THF was slowly added into the flask. After stirring for 2 h, all solvents were removed by reduced pressure. The residues were further purified by preparative HPLC to afford G0-6C-AA for the following synthesis. 1H NMR (400 MHz, DMSO-*d*6:CDCl3 = 1:1, Supplementary Fig. 44A) δ 7.50 (d, *J* = 7.7 Hz, 1H), 7.21 (d, *J* = 8.2 Hz, 1H), 7.10 – 6.83 (m, 4H), 6.17 (s, 1H), 4.10 (s, 1H), 3.88 (t, *J* = 18.1 Hz, 4H), 3.66 (s, 3H), 3.42 – 2.90 (m, 16H), 2.78 (s, 4H), 2.62 (s, 6H), 2.40 (s, 4H), 2.22 (s, 8H), 1.49 (s, 4H), 1.29 (s, 4H). 13C NMR (101 MHz, DMSO-*d*6/CDCl3 = 1/1, Supplementary Fig. 44B) δ 173.52, 170.95, 158.17, 154.61, 136.32, 129.35, 127.47, 121.65, 119.77, 118.54, 111.17, 109.24, 64.14, 56.53, 51.87, 50.21, 38.19, 35.21, 32.22, 28.84, 27.51, 24.42. LC-MS (m/z, Supplementary Fig. 45): Calcd for [M+H]+: 905.6, found: 905.8; Calcd for [M+2H]2+: 453.3, found: 453.6; Calcd for [M+3H]3+: 302.5, found: 302.8.
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:194:*Synthesis of G0-6C-AA-C12*. G0-6C-AA (1 equiv.) and 1,2-epoxydodecane (7.2 equiv.) were dissolved in ethanol in a glass vial. The reaction mixture was stirred at 70 oC for 12 h. (**B-D**) Tumour growth and mice survival were monitored. Data are presented as mean ± SD from *n*=5 biologically independent samples. Statistical differences in **B** were calculated using two-way ANOVA with Tukey's multiple comparisons test. Survival analysis in **D** was performed using a log-rank (Mantel–Cox) test (*n*=5 biologically independent samples).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:590:**Supplementary Fig. 87**. Evaluation of the systemic toxicity of pLNPs after intravenous or intratumoural injection. (**A**) Mice were subcutaneously injected with MC38 cells on day 0. Free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP plus free IDO inhibitor, or G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP were administered either intravenous or intratumoural injection on day 6, day 8, and day 10. PBS served as negative control. The serum of mice from each treatment group was collected on day 12. (**B-G**) IL-6, TNF-α, and IFN-γ levels in serum after intravenous or intratumoural injection of each treatment. (**H-J**) Comparison of IL-6, TNF-α, and IFN-γ levels in serum after intravenous or intratumoural injection of G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP. Data are presented as mean ± SD from *n=*3 biologically independent samples. Statistical differences were calculated using one-way ANOVA with Tukey's multiple comparisons test in B-G, and Student's t-tests in H-J (ns indicated no significance).
F:\내 드라이브\EXTRACT-TEST\QS_2026_3\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM\41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM.md:594:**Supplementary Fig. 88**. AST and ALT levels after intravenous injection of pLNP or control therapeutics. Tumour-bearing mice were intravenously injected with free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP plus free IDO inhibitor, or G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP. PBS was injected as a negative control. AST and ALT levels were quantified via ELISA. Data are presented as mean ± SD from *n=*3 biologically independent samples. Statistical differences were calculated using one-way ANOVA with Dunnett's multiple comparisons test (ns indicated no significance).

codex
I found 71 selected rows. The main article’s extracted images are full multi-panel figure crops keyed by Marker page IDs, while supplementary figures appear as one image per supplementary PDF page; I’m now checking the markdown ordering so page numbers and image names line up before writing the JSON.

from pathlib import Path
paper=Path(os.environ['PAPER_FOLDER'])
for sub in ['QS_2026','41565_2025_2102_MOESM1_ESM']:
    print('---',sub)
    for p in sorted((paper/sub).glob('*.jpeg')):
        m=re.search(r'_page_(\\d+)_(Figure|Picture)_(\\d+)',p.name)
        print(p.name)

Messenger RNA-based immunotherapy has emerged as a promising treatment for cancer  $^{1-4}$ ; however, cancer-associated T cell exhaustion may hinder effectiveness  $^{5,6}$ . While combining mRNA immunotherapy with immune checkpoint blockade can overcome this challenge  $^{6-8}$ , treatment resistance and low immune checkpoint blockade response rates limit response to a subset of patients  $^{9,10}$ . Thus, there is a critical need to develop immunotherapies that target novel immunosuppressive pathways. For example, indoleamine 2,3-dioxygenase (IDO) is a highly expressed enzyme in cancer cells  $^{11-14}$  that induces effector T cell exhaustion and facilitates cancer immune evasion  $^{15,16}$ . One IDO inhibitor  $^{17-21}$ , indoximod (1-methyl-D-tryptophan), is being evaluated in clinical trials

for solid tumour therapy (NCT03301636, NCT02077881)  $^{22-24}$ . In addition, activation of naive T cells via cytokine-based immunotherapy, such as interleukin-12 (IL-12), is the central point of several clinical trials for treating a range of cancers  $^{25-27}$ . However, systemic administration of IL-12 is poorly tolerated, rapidly increasing pro-inflammatory cytokines and potentially inducing a life-threatening cytokine storm  $^{28-31}$ . In this study, we sought to synergize IDO inhibitor-mediated reversal of T cell exhaustion with local IL-12-mediated T cell activation as a synergistic strategy for solid tumour therapy.

Co-delivery of these therapeutic molecules to the tumour microenvironment (TME) may be achieved via direct intratumoural

A full list of affiliations appears at the end of the paper. Me-mail: mjmitch@seas.upenn.edu

![](_page_1_Figure_2.jpeg)

<span id="page-1-0"></span>Fig. 1 | Engineering IDO inhibitor-based prodrug ionizable lipids (pILs) to facilitate IL-12 mRNA delivery via prodrug lipid nanoparticles (pLNPs) for synergistic cancer immunotherapy. a, Overview of the pIL synthesis route, including the seven prodrug amine heads and three epoxide tails used to synthesize pILs. The disulfide linkage in the pIL can be triggered by intracellular GSH to release IDO inhibitor into the cytoplasm. b, Scheme showing

intratumoural injection of pLNPs, which are composed of plLs, phospholipid, DMG-PEG, cholesterol and IL-12 mRNA. pLNPs induce T cell activation and suppress T cell exhaustion through IL-12 expression and IDO pathway inhibitor release, respectively, thereby eliminating tumours through synergistic methods. Panel  $\bf b$  created in BioRender; Hamilton, A. https://biorender.com/bv1w20v (2025).

administration of lipid nanoparticles (LNPs)<sup>32</sup>. Conventional LNPs are composed of ionizable lipids, phospholipids, cholesterol and polyethylene glycol (PEG)-conjugated lipids<sup>4,33,34</sup>. While LNPs are highly effective for mRNA delivery, the loading efficiency of free small-molecule drugs in LNPs is low and risks drug leakage in vivo<sup>35-37</sup>. By contrast, prodrug-based nanosystems have higher drug loading capacity, good biocompatibility and controlled drug release in vivo<sup>38-41</sup>. Here we engineered prodrug ionizable lipids (pILs) conjugated to indoximod via a glutathione (GSH)-cleavable disulfide linkage<sup>41,42</sup> (Fig. 1a). pILs were used to formulate prodrug LNPs (pLNPs) encapsulating IL-12 mRNA,

which demonstrated excellent primary and distal anti-tumour efficacy along with protection against tumour rechallenge in a mouse model of colon adenocarcinoma (Fig. 1b). More broadly, pLNPs offer a generalizable strategy to engineer small-molecule-conjugated mRNA-LNP platforms for cancer immunotherapy.

# Synthesis of pIL library for in vitro and in vivo screening

Conventional ionizable lipids were synthesized via an  $S_N^2$  reaction between polyamine heads and epoxide-terminated lipid tails<sup>43</sup>. To

<span id="page-2-0"></span>**Fig. 2**| **In vitro and in vivo screening of pIL library and lead pLNPs for mRNA delivery. a**, Schematic showing screening method for pILs. pLNPs were formulated with a pIL, cholesterol, DOPE and DMG-PEG at a molar ratio of 50:38.5:10:1.5. MC38 cells were used for in vitro screening of pLNPs encapsulating FLuc mRNA. Mice were intratumourally injected with G0-SS-AA-C10/C12/C14 FLuc pLNPs and MC3 FLuc LNP at an mRNA dosage

of 2.5  $\mu$ g per mouse for 6 h and the total flux was quantified. Data are presented as mean  $\pm$  s.d. Panel **a** created in BioRender; Hamilton, A. https://biorender.com/h01uj00 (2025).

synthesize an indoximod pIL, we first generated an intermediate prodrug structure, Nitro-SS-AA (Supplementary Fig. 1). As shown in Supplementary Fig. 1, the hydroxyls of 2-hydroxyethyl disulfide were first protected by 4-nitrophenyl carbonochloridate to generate reactive carbonates (Supplementary Figs. 1 and 4) for a subsequent reaction

with indoximod (1-methyl-D-tryptophan) to obtain 4-Nitro-SS-AA (Supplementary Fig. 5). The presence of a disulfide linkage in this structure enables intracellular release of indoximod via GSH cleavage. Next, Nitro-SS-AA was reacted with seven amine heads to generate seven prodrug polyamine heads, and all structures were confirmed by

NMR and electrospray ionization mass spectrometry (ESI-MS) spectra (Fig. [1a](#page-1-0) and Supplementary Figs. 2 and 6??9). These structures were then reacted with 1 of the 3 epoxide-terminated alkyl tails for 12?뎗 at 70?됀캜 to yield a library of 21 pILs (Supplementary Fig. 3), and all structures of pILs were confirmed by NMR and high resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) spectra (Supplementary Figs. 20??9). In this study, pILs are denoted by a specific nomenclature: P2A to DAB refers to the seven amine head groups, SS refers to the presence of a disulfide linkage, AA refers to the presence of 1-methyl-D-tryptophan, and C10/ C12/C14 refers to the length of the alkyl tails in the ionizable lipid. For example, 'G0-SS-AA-C12' has a G0 amine head, a disulfide linkage, 1-methyl-D-tryptophan and C12 alkyl tails.

Next, the pILs were combined with 1,2-dioleoyl-sn-glycero-3-p hosphoethanolamine (DOPE), 1,2-dimyristoyl-rac-glycero-3-m ethoxypolyethylene glycol-2000 (DMG-PEG) and cholesterol in ethanol and pipette-mixed with 1-methylpseudouridine (m1?)-modified firefly luciferase (FLuc) mRNA to formulate 21 pLNPs. The molar ratio of pIL/cholesterol/DOPE/DMG-PEG was kept constant at 50:38.5:10:1.5 (Fig. [2a](#page-2-0)). The pLNP library was screened in HepG2 cells, an immortalized human hepatocellular carcinoma cell line. Among the 21 pILs, G0-based pILs had the highest transfection efficiency in vitro among the 7 amine head groups (Supplementary Fig. 47). The top eight pILs were advanced for study of in vivo efficiency following intravenous administration of pLNPs encapsulating FLuc mRNA at a dose of 2.5?됌펓 per mouse. All pLNPs primarily transfected the liver. G0-based pLNPs (G0-SS-AA-C10/ C12/C14) exhibited higher expression of reporter protein compared with other LNPs, including LNPs containing the ionizable lipid MC3, which is used in US Food and Drug Administration (FDA)-approved formulations (Supplementary Fig. 48).

Then, we verified that pILs could be used to transfect an additional cancer cell line. We repeated a similar workflow, this time using MC38 cells, a mouse colon adenocarcinoma cell line (Fig. [2a\)](#page-2-0). Treatment of MC38 cells in vitro with our library of pLNPs encapsulating FLuc mRNA showed that G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP had the highest transfection efficiency (Fig. [2b\)](#page-2-0). All pLNPs exhibited low cytotoxicity in vitro (Supplementary Fig. 49). To emphasize the structural advantage of the pIL, we also designed a control lipid, termed G0-6C-AA-C12, that could not release drug by substituting the disulfide linkage with carbon?밹arbon linkage (Supplementary Figs. 40??6). Interestingly, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP facilitated 1.5-fold greater transfection of MC38 cells than LNPs formulated with a structural analogue that lacked the disulfide linkage (G0-6C-AA-C12) (Supplementary Fig. 50). Since G0-6C-AA-C12 has a carbon?밹arbon bond instead of a disulfide linkage, it is unable to release indoximod in the cytoplasm, implying that the prodrug itself within the pIL potentially aids in pLNP-mediated mRNA transfection.

The top three pILs (G0-SS-AA-C10, G0-SS-AA-C12 and G0-SS-AA-C14) from our initial screens were advanced for further study. Given our interest in applying pLNPs for solid tumour therapy, we next assessed pIL efficiency relative to two control lipids (G0-6C-AA-C12 and MC3) after intratumoural injection into mouse MC38 tumours. In brief, LNPs encapsulating FLuc mRNA at a dose of 2.5?됌펓 per mouse were administered to MC38 tumour-bearing mice, and luciferase expression was quantified 6?뎗 post-injection using bioluminescence imaging (Fig. [2c](#page-2-0) and Supplementary Fig. 51). G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP facilitated greater whole-body mRNA transfection compared with MC3 LNP (Supplementary Fig. 51). Ex vivo quantification of luminescence intensity specifically within MC38 tumours demonstrated that G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP outperformed other pLNPs and MC3 LNP (Fig. [2c\)](#page-2-0). Moreover, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP induced low off-target transfection of the liver, while MC3 LNP induced substantially higher liver transfection (Supplementary Fig. 51). On the basis of these data, G0-SS-AA-C12 was selected as the lead pIL for further study.

Purified G0-SS-AA-C12 or G0-6C-AA-C12 were combined with standard excipients and mixed with FLuc mRNA using a microfluidic device to generate pLNPs for physiochemical characterization. Transmission electron microscopy images showed uniform, multilamellar, complex spherical morphologies of both G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP with hydrodynamic diameters of 43?뎝m and 78?뎝m, polydispersity index values of 0.12 and 0.26, and slightly positive surface charges (Supplementary Figs. 52 and 53). FLuc mRNA encapsulation efficiency (EE) of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP was determined to be 91.2% and 89.6%, respectively, and their pKa values were approximately 6.67 and 6.50, respectively (Supplementary Figs. 52??4). Next, we compared the cellular uptake of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP and MC3 LNP via 3,3??dioctadecyloxacarbocyanine perchlorate (DiO) labelling. Confocal imaging showed greater intracellular uptake of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP relative to MC3 and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNPs in MC38 cells (Supplementary Fig. 55). Moreover, we assessed the endosomal escape of the lead pLNP. A higher percentage of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP achieved endosomal escape compared with MC3 LNP in MC38 cells (Fig. [2d](#page-2-0)), demonstrating a potential mechanism for enhanced efficiency for the lead delivery platform.

#### **pLNP packaging IL-12 mRNA limits T cell exhaustion in vitro**

Encouraged by the strong in vitro and in vivo efficiency of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP, we next assessed its potential to deliver IL-12 mRNA. We formulated G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP and MC3 LNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA and treated MC38 cells at a dose of 0.5?됌펓?뎜l?? of mRNA. After 16?뎗, both the supernatant and cells were collected for enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA)-based analysis of extracellular and intracellular IL-12 protein expression (Fig. [3a\)](#page-3-0). Although both extracellular and intracellular IL-12 protein was detected in all three treatment groups, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP facilitated 44-fold greater extracellular IL-12 protein and 8.6-fold greater intracellular IL-12 protein than MC3 LNP (Fig. [3b,c](#page-3-0)). Moreover, treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP resulted in 2.8-fold greater extracellular IL-12 protein and 4.4-fold greater intracellular IL-12 protein than G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP (Fig. [3b,c\)](#page-3-0). Flow cytometry (Supplementary Fig. 56) and confocal imaging confirmed strong intracellular IL-12 expression in MC38 cells after G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP treatment (Fig. [3d,e\)](#page-3-0). We then investigated the drug release profile of G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP. Specifically, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNPs

We subsequently explored the potential of GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP to promote T cell activation and limit T cell exhaustion via an in vitro serial culture assay (Supplementary Figs. 60-63). In brief, MC38 cells were treated with PBS (negative control) or GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating either IL-12 mRNA or FLuc mRNA. After 24 h, the supernatant of each group was collected and used to culture primary mouse CD3<sup>+</sup>T cells. Following 48 h of culture, T cells were collected and analysed for production of key effector cytokines, including tumour necrosis factor-慣  $(TNF-\alpha)$ , interferon-y (IFNy) and granzyme B (Supplementary Fig. 60a). Cytokine levels were measured via intracellular cytokine staining and flow cytometry. CD3<sup>+</sup> T cells cultured in supernatant from MC38 cells treated with GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP exhibited notably higher levels of TNF- $\alpha$ , IFNy and granzyme B compared with those cultured in control supernatants (Supplementary Fig. 60). Moreover, treatment with GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP enhanced cytokine production in CD4<sup>+</sup> and CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells (Supplementary Figs. 61 and 62) and enhanced T cell proliferation (Supplementary Fig. 67).

The capacity of GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP to mitigate T cell exhaustion was evaluated by co-culturing primary OT-I CD3<sup>+</sup> T cells with ovalbumin-modified MC38 (MC38-OVA) cancer cells at a 1:1 effector-to-target (E:T) ratio. GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP and GO-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP were added at 0.5 쨉g per well for 3 days. Cells treated with PBS served as a negative control. Every 3 days, equivalent numbers of MC38-OVA cells and corresponding amounts of mRNApLNP were added, for a total of 5 cycles, to mimic chronic T cell stimulation within the TME. After the final stimulation, cells were collected and analysed via flow cytometry for CD69 and CD25 expression, markers of T cell activation, and for PD-1 expression, a marker of T cell exhaustion<sup>5,46</sup> (Fig. 3h). Treatment with GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP upregulated CD69 and CD25 on OT-I CD3+T cells (Fig. 3i,j and Supplementary Figs. 64a,b and 65) and downregulated PD-1 (Fig. 3k and Supplementary Figs. 64c and 66) relative to control groups. Interestingly, GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA mediated greater suppression of PD-1 expression than pLNP encapsulating FLuc mRNA (Fig. 3k and Supplementary Fig. 64c). Together, these results imply a synergistic mechanism between the released indoximod and translated IL-12 in resisting T cell exhaustion.

# pLNP delivering IL-12 mRNA enables MC38 tumour immunotherapy

We next evaluated the therapeutic efficacy of GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP in an MC38 tumour mouse model. To highlight the synergistic effect of this platform, composed of both a small-molecule IDO inhibitor and therapeutic IL-12 mRNA, we prepared two control LNPs. GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating FLuc mRNA was used to assess the effect of IDO inhibitor only, while GO-6C-AA-C12 LNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA was used to evaluate the anti-tumour efficacy of IL-12 mRNA only (Supplementary Fig. 68). After subcutaneous injection of MC38 cells into the right flank of mice, GO-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, GO-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP or GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP were administered intratumourally at an mRNA dose of 2.5 쨉g per mouse on days 6, 8 and 10 (Fig. 4a). PBS was administered via the same procedure as a negative control. GO-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA suppressed tumour

<span id="page-6-0"></span>**Fig. 5** | **Characterization of TME after treatment with GO-SS-AA IL-12 pLNPs encapsulating IL-12 mRNA. a**, Schematic showing establishment of the MC38 tumour model for flow cytometry and bulk RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) after treatment. Mice bearing MC38 tumours received intratumoural injection of PBS, GO-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, GO-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP or GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP. On day 12, tumours in each treatment group were collected, and a portion of tumour tissues was digested into a single-cell suspension for analysis of T cell populations via flow cytometry, while the remaining cells were digested for RNA-seq of tumour tissues. **b,c**, Quantification of CD8\* T cells (**b**) and CD4\* T cells (**c**) among CD3\* T cells in tumour tissues after different treatments (n = 5). **d,e**, Analysis of PD-1 expression on the surface of CD8\* T cells (**d**) and CD4\* T cells (**e**) (n = 5). **f,g**, Cytokine levels of TNF-慣 (**f**) and IFNy (**g**) in tumour tissues (n = 3).

growth (Fig. 4b), inducing compelete tumour regression in all treated mice within 31 days of model initiation (Fig. 4b,c). In comparison, G0-SS-AA-C12 pLNP encapsulating FLuc mRNA and G0-6C-AA-C12 LNP encapsulating IL-12 mRNA only partially inhibited tumour growth (Fig. 4b,c). All LNPs were well tolerated with no effect on body weight (Fig. 4d) or major organ histology (Supplementary Fig. 69) relative to control mice. Finally, PBS-treated mice succumbed to tumour burden before day 45, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP treatment moderately increased survival, and all G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP-treated mice survived to our study endpoint (Fig. 4e).

To further characterize the importance of co-formulation of the IDO inhibitor with IL-12 mRNA in a single LNP, we evaluated three additional control groups: free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP with free IDO inhibitor (Supplementary Fig. 70a). GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP demonstrated superior anti-tumour efficacy compared to these control groups. Neither free IL-12 mRNA nor free IDO inhibitor alone notably suppressed tumour growth (Supplementary Fig. 70b,e). G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP plus free IDO inhibitor only partially inhibited tumour progression with mean tumour volumes rising rapidly after day 22 (Supplementary Fig. 70b,c). By contrast, GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP completely suppressed tumour growth, and achieved complete regression of all tumours within 32 days in this study (Supplementary Fig. 70c,e). All mice in the GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP-treated group survived to study endpoint (Supplementary Fig. 70d). Splenic CD4+, CD8+ and regulatory T cell response was also assessed for each of these groups on day 12 (Supplementary Fig. 71a). Notably, GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP elicited a robust CD8<sup>+</sup>T cell response and diminished the number of CD4<sup>+</sup> and regulatory T cells compared with control groups (Supplementary Figs. 71-73). Overall, these data confirmed that co-formulation of both an IDO inhibitor and IL-12 mRNA in a single LNP is integral to induction of a potent immune response and anti-tumour efficacy.

We next investigated the anti-tumour mechanism of G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP. Given that IL-12 is known to activate cells in both the innate and adaptive immune compartment and the spleen plays a major role in immunoregulation  $^{47,48}$ , we more specifically explored the effect of pLNPs specifically on splenic T cell populations in the MC38 tumour mouse model. As observed previously, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP biased splenic T cells towards CD8 $^{+}$  populations and away from CD4 $^{+}$  populations relative to control groups (Fig. 4f?밾). More specifically, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP enhanced effector memory (CD44 $^{+}$ CD62L $^{-}$ ) and central memory (CD44 $^{+}$ CD62L $^{+}$ ) CD4 $^{+}$  and CD8 $^{+}$  splenic T cell populations (Fig. 4i?뱇 and Supplementary Figs. 74 and 75), and reduced the presence of splenic regulatory T cells (CD4 $^{+}$ CD25 $^{+}$ FOXP3 $^{+}$ ) (Fig. 4m and Supplementary Figs. 76 and 77). Together, these results demonstrate that G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP achieves anti-tumour efficacy via immune priming towards an effector T cell phenotype while also generating long-term immune memory.

# pLNP drives effector T cell infiltration and TME activation

Having characterized the impact of GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP on splenic immunoregulation, we next evaluated the mechanisms of anti-tumour efficacy in MC38 tumours. A subcutaneous MC38 tumour mouse model

was prepared and treated with either PBS, GO-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, GO-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP or GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP as described previously. On day 12, tumours were collected and digested into single-cell suspensions (Fig. 5a). GO-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP increased CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells

within the TME (Fig. 5b) relative to control groups, likely owing to IL-12-mediated recruitment and stimulation of T cell effector functions. There was no significant difference between the number of CD4 $^+$  cells between treatment groups (Fig. 5c). In line with the observed increase

![](_page_7_Figure_4.jpeg)

in T cell infiltration, intracellular levels of TNF-慣, IFN款 and granzyme B in CD3<sup>+</sup> T cells were elevated after treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP (Supplementary Fig. 78a,b,d?밿). Relative to PBS-treated tumours, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP downregulated PD-1 expression on CD4+ and CD8<sup>+</sup> T cells, demonstrating the influence of the released IDO inhibitor (Fig. [5d,e\)](#page-6-0). G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP further decreased CD4<sup>+</sup> and CD8<sup>+</sup> T cell exhaustion in the TME, demonstrating further synergy in therapeutic function between IL-12 and IDO inhibitor (Fig. [5d,e](#page-6-0) and Supplementary Figs. 81 and 82). G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP also significantly elevated pro-inflammatory cytokines IL-6, TNF-慣 and IFN款 levels in tumour tissues (Fig. [5f,g](#page-6-0) and Supplementary Fig. 78a?밹). Finally, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP maintained performance relative to tumours treated with free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP with free IDO inhibitor (Supplementary Figs. 79 and 80), highlighting the importance of IDO inhibitor and IL-12 mRNA co-formulation. Thus, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP enhances effector T cell infiltration and reduces T cell exhaustion in the TME via synergistic mechanisms, leading to the anti-tumour efficacy observed in the MC38 tumour mouse model.

To understand the impact of IDO inhibitor and IL-12 release from pLNPs on the gene expression profile of cells within the TME, we performed bulk RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) on collected MC38 tumours (Fig. [5a,h?뱋](#page-6-0)). IL-12 exerts many of its downstream effects via IFN[款49.](#page-11-27) Thus, it was unsurprising that both G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP- and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP-treated tumours had an upregulation of IFN款 transcripts by 6.3-fold and 2.1-fold, respectively, relative to PBS-treated tumours (Fig. [5i](#page-6-0)), which was consistent with high IFN款 levels in tumour tissue (Fig. [5g\)](#page-6-0). Twenty-eight pro-inflammatory cytokine genes were significantly enriched following treatment with G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP (Fig. [5j](#page-6-0) and Supplementary Fig. 83a), while G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP induced expression of 11 and 4 genes, respectively (Supplementary Figs. 84 and 85). At a macro level, G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP shifted the gene expression profile of the TME from 'cold' to 'hot', evidenced by upregulation of leukocyte chemokines and pathways responsible for T cell priming, dendritic cell (DC) activation, nucleotide-binding oligomerization domain (NOD)-like receptor signalling, cancer cell apoptosis, and T cell and natural killer (NK) cell activation (Fig. [5j?뱋](#page-6-0)). By contrast, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP and G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP induced only modest changes in immune-response genes (Supplementary Figs. 83b,c, 84 and 85). These data imply that G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNPs modulate the TME by exerting immunostimulatory effects that lead to the anti-tumour efficacy of this platform.

#### **Anti-tumour efficacy and safety of pLNP after systemic dosing**

Given the robust anti-tumour efficacy of G0-SS-AA-C12 IL-12 pLNP after intratumoural administration, we further explored the efficacy of pLNP after intravenous administration in a mouse MC38 tumour model. Mice were treated with free IL-12 mRNA, free IDO inhibitor, G0-SS-AA-C12 FLuc pLNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12 LNP, G0-6C-AA-C12 IL-12
